        var $userConsent = "0";
    }
    try{
        docereeAds.add("userDetails", {
            "userconsent": $userConsent,
            "specialization": "Pediatrics",
        })
    }
    catch(err){
        console.log(err)
    }
</script>

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="f72c36a6b40a47b6a3a0c3498442ff2f"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="f72c36a6b40a47b6a3a0c3498442ff2f">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'c665a0e7-04a7-4622-bca9-ddc837a0de18'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d91677038" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p><b>Hierarchical Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T</b><sub><i><b>x</b></i></sub><b>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC composite</b>: The flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC combining the advantages of high electrical conductivity and cycling stability of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> and the high specific capacity of MoS<sub>2</sub> were prepared by a combination of complexation, polymerization and sulfidation reactions. Its use as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries demonstrated excellent electrochemical performance.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0d66fd66-3364-4e5c-ac15-2ba6f335ea1e/batt202300391-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0d66fd66-3364-4e5c-ac15-2ba6f335ea1e/batt202300391-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/398844af-792b-45ad-b512-d4626042421e/batt202300391-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0d66fd66-3364-4e5c-ac15-2ba6f335ea1e/batt202300391-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d91677041" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The lack of high-performance electrodes is a key factor restricting the development of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), and usually a certain type of electrode material alone often fails to combine the merits of high capacity and good conductivity. Herein, nitrogen-doped carbon (NC) was used as a multifunctional bridge to uniformly compound high-capacity MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles with highly conductive Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets to form Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC composite with flower-like architecture. Ultrafine MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles were uniformly anchored on Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets to form nanosheets primary building blocks with a sandwich-like architecture, which not only shortens the ion transfer distance but also enlarges the layer spacing of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> favoring the storage of more sodium ions as well as their transport capacity. Moreover, the flower-like structures with abundant edges can provide an ample active sites and high pseudocapacitance behavior. As a result, flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC displays a high electrochemical properties with an excellent cycling stability over 2000 cycles (81.4 % capacity retention) and preeminent rate capacity of 383 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC‖Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell displays a capacity of 59 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.4 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 180 cycles. This work displays a high-performance anode composite for SIBs and provides insight into the preparation of other composites.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="batt202300391-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="batt202300391-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In the face of growing energy demand and serious environmental pollution problems, the use of green renewable energy is imperative, and electrochemical energy storage technology is one of the critical technologies to address the intermittency and volatility of renewable energy.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0001_R_d91677030e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Rev.">1</a>-<a href="#batt202300391-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0003_R_d91677030e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span> Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are commonly used because of their satisfactory performance, which has led to the depletion of limited lithium mining resources, and therefore the cost of LIBs has been rising.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0004_R_d91677030e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#batt202300391-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0005_R_d91677030e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Commun.">5</a></sup></span> As a result, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have received particular attention in recent years because sodium has similar properties to lithium but is more abundant and cheaper.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0006_R_d91677030e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Energy">6</a>-<a href="#batt202300391-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0008_R_d91677030e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Mate">8</a></sup></span> Since the weight and radius of Na<sup>+</sup> is larger than that of Li<sup>+</sup>, graphite, a commercial LIB anode material with a small layer spacing, is not suitable for sodium storage.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0009_R_d91677030e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#batt202300391-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0010_R_d91677030e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ChemSusChem.">10</a></sup></span> In addition, the large size of the Na<sup>+</sup> inevitably leads to hysteresis reaction kinetics and a tremendous volume expansion of the electrode materials during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0011_R_d91677030e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">11</a>-<a href="#batt202300391-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0013_R_d91677030e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Nano">13</a></sup></span> Therefore, it becomes critical to explore novel high-performance anode materials to facilitate the early commercial production application of SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>To improve the performance of SIBs, various alloying-type materials,<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0014_R_d91677030e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a>-<a href="#batt202300391-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0017_R_d91677030e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Mater.">17</a></sup></span> transition metal oxides,<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0018_R_d91677030e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Energy">18</a>-<a href="#batt202300391-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0020_R_d91677030e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Energy Storage">20</a></sup></span> transition metal sulfides,<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0021_R_d91677030e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Fiber Mater.">21</a>-<a href="#batt202300391-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0024_R_d91677030e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span> and other anode materials have been investigated. Among them, MXenes have attracted tremendous interest due to their ultra-high conductivity, unique layer structure, wide layer spacing and abundant tunable termination.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0025_R_d91677030e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Sci.">25</a>-<a href="#batt202300391-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0028_R_d91677030e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Eng. J.">28</a></sup></span> However, since MXene is a insertion-type material, it can provide a lower specific capacity compared to alloying-type and conversion-type materials, and the susceptibility to re-stacking between neighboring nanosheets leads to a further reduction in its surface utilization, which seriously hampers its practical application.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0029_R_d91677030e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nano Energy">29</a>, <a href="#batt202300391-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0030_R_d91677030e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Power Sources">30</a></sup></span> The compounding of MXenes with high-capacity materials not only increases the specific capacity of whole composites but also improves the stacking resistance of MXenes. Various MXene based composites have been successfully prepared and exhibit improved electrochemical performances.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0031_R_d91677030e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Nano">31</a>-<a href="#batt202300391-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0035_R_d91677030e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Mater. Today Energy">35</a></sup></span> Yuan et al.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0031_R_d91677030e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Nano">31</a></sup></span> obtained 3D cross-linked Nb<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>@C hybrid by hydrothermal anchoring of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets on the Nb<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> framework combined with PDA-derived carbon coating strategies. It displayed a high stability and capacity of 530 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles with an ultra-low degradation of 0.01 % for per cycle. Yuan et al.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0032_R_d91677030e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Chem. Eng. J.">32</a></sup></span> wrapped Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> on the surface of NiSe<sub>2</sub>@C spheres by an electrostatic assembly strategy, which effectively prevented the stacking of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> and thus improved its electrochemical properties. Han et al.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0033_R_d91677030e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Chem. Eng. J.">33</a></sup></span> prepared few-layered Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>/CoS<sub>2</sub>@NPC with superior rate capability and long-term stability by carbonization and sulfurization treatment of f-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>/ZIF-67 composites. Yuan et al.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0034_R_d91677030e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Chem. Eng. J.">34</a></sup></span> synthesized Nb<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>@CS architectures with high capacity and rate performance by hydrothermal and electrostatic self-assembly approaches. Liu et al.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0035_R_d91677030e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Mater. Today Energy">35</a></sup></span> successfully prepared the hierarchical 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub>/MXene heterostructure with large interlayer spacing and highly conductive by solvothermal method. The above results indicate that compositing MXene with high specific capacity materials to form composites can effectively improve the sodium storage performance of MXene.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we successfully anchored ultrafine MoS<sub>2</sub> with high theoretical specific capacity of 670 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> on the surface of MXene nanosheet material with high electrical conductivity and cyclic stability to form a flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC composite by a combination of complexation, polymerization and sulfidation reactions. The complexation of molybdate with DA ensures a homogeneous compounding of the molybdenum source with DA and the nanosizing of the molybdenum source, which not only increases in material utilization but also effectively mitigates the volume variation during sodiation/desodiation progress. The polymerization reaction of DA in a weak alkaline environment firmly anchors the Mo source on the surface of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets forming a sandwich-like architecture, which not only expands the layer spacing of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> for rapid ion transport but also effectively prevents its stacking. After the specific sulfidation reaction, the molybdenum source was successfully converted into MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles and PDA into nitrogen-doped carbon (NC) as a linker to ensure good contact between MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets to obtain high electrical conductivity. Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> acts as the active material in the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC complex while also acting as a skeleton support as well as providing a large active surface and high pseudocapacitive behavior. The as-prepared Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC composite combine the advantages of MoS<sub>2</sub> and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>, combining high electrical conductivity and specific capacity with excellent cycling stability. Thus, it shows excellent rate property for sodium storage with a capacity of 383 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Meanwhile, it also exhibits stable cycling performance with a capacity of 351 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 2000 cycles at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The corresponding capacity retention rate from the 20th cycle to the 2000th cycle is 81.4 % with an extremely low capacity fade of 0.0103 % per cycle.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="batt202300391-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="batt202300391-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0001">1</a> proposes the synthesis process of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC composite. Firstly, Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets with larger size and fewer layers were successfully prepared by etching the Al atomic layers from Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub>. Then, the nano-sized Mo source was anchored on the surface of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets to form Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@Mo/PDA nanosheets with sandwich-like structure by complexation of molybdate with DA and polymerization of DA. Finally, the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@Mo/PDA precursors were vulcanized to form Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC at high temperature in N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="batt202300391-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ea9db97a-0e8b-4df7-b158-4abf6c86a12f/batt202300391-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ea9db97a-0e8b-4df7-b158-4abf6c86a12f/batt202300391-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3908a4dc-af65-419b-b2bd-938f9720b2f5/batt202300391-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ea9db97a-0e8b-4df7-b158-4abf6c86a12f/batt202300391-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202300391-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202300391" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Schematic illustration of the preparation of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC composite.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure S1 shows that the sizes of the prepared few-layer Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets are concentrated in the range of tens to hundreds of microns. 2D Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets provide a large surface area which facilitates their surface utilization and the loading of other materials with high capacity on their surface. The Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@Mo/PDA primary building blocks with sandwich-like structure were formed by successfully anchoring the ultrafine Mo source on the surface of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets using the polymerization and coating effect of DA. The formation of a sandwich-like structure not only expands the layer spacing of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> to improve ion transport channels but also prevents Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> stacking. The addition of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> has an essential effect on the morphology of the precursor, as presented in Figure S2. When the addition amount of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> was 10 mg, the obtained precursors exhibit flower-like structures with sizes concentrated in 1–3 μm, and the thickness of their primary nanosheet assembly units was significantly thicker than pure Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> (Figure S2a, b) this is due to the loading of a layer of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles on the surface of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>. Increasing the addition of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> to 20 mg, the flower-like structure of the resulting Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@Mo/PDA precursor became less obvious (Figure S2c, d). When the introduction of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> was increased to 40 mg, the resulting product was in the form of tightly packed nanosheets rather than flower-like structure (Figure S2e, f). More importantly, the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@Mo/PDA precursor has good structural thermal stability and maintains its original morphology and structure after high temperature vulcanization to Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC (Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0002">2a</a>, <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0002">b</a> and Figure S3). In view of the low theoretical specific capacity of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> and the best flower-like morphological structure of the product obtained when 10 mg of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> was introduced, the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC was investigated in detail later.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="batt202300391-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7ba32da7-8d08-4ac1-bb9e-c312624039e5/batt202300391-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7ba32da7-8d08-4ac1-bb9e-c312624039e5/batt202300391-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/563353a9-f25e-4abd-9406-501cce889766/batt202300391-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7ba32da7-8d08-4ac1-bb9e-c312624039e5/batt202300391-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202300391-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202300391" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a, b) SEM images, c, d) TEM images, e) HRTEM image, f) SAED pattern, g–l) TEM element mapping of the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The SEM images of the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC show that the assembled units are nanosheets and their thickness is significantly larger than that of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> (Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0002">2a</a>, <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0002">b</a>). The increase of the nanosheet thickness indicates the successful cladding of MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC layers on the surface of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanoflakes. The flower-like structure formed by interweaving nanosheets can expose more active surfaces and offer substantial sodium storage sites, while providing a smooth transport channel for Na<sup>+</sup> transport.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0037_R_d91677030e1052" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">37</a></sup></span> The TEM results at low magnification are consistent with the SEM results, from which flower-like structures with rich margins can also be observed (Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0002">2c</a>, <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0002">d</a>). The clear lattice stripes with a spacing of 0.62 nm can be viewed from the high-resolution TEM image of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC indicating the good crystallinity of the complex (Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0002">2e</a>). This distance is consistent with the (002) lattice spacing of MoS<sub>2</sub>,<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0038_R_d91677030e1077" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Sci.">38</a></sup></span> indicating that the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles are successfully anchored to the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets, which is consistent with the increased thickness of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets shown by the SEM results (Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0002">2b</a>). In addition, it can be observed in Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0002">2</a>(e) that the amorphous NC and superfine MoS<sub>2</sub> are uniform compounded on the surface of the few-layer Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanoflakes, which not only enhances the utilisation of MoS<sub>2</sub> and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> but also effectively increases the conductivity of the overall electrode material. The characteristic diffraction rings of MoS<sub>2</sub> can be observed in the corresponding selected area electron diffraction (SAED), indicating that the prepared MoS<sub>2</sub> has polycrystalline structural characteristics (Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0002">2f</a>). The EDS elemental distribution images of the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC display an even distribution of five elements Mo, S, Ti, C and N, indicating that the MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC layers are uniformly compounded with the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets (Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0002">2g</a>–<a href="#batt202300391-fig-0002">l</a>). The close contact between MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets and the homogeneous composite contribute to the enhancement of the specific capacity and electrical conductivity of the electrode materials. In addition, the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC hierarchical structure is assembled from nanosheets with sandwich-like structure formed by porous NC anchoring ultra-small MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles on the surface of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets, and thus it has a large specific surface area of 118 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> as well as abundant mesoporous structure, which is conducive for it to provide a high surface pseudocapacitance behavior as well as a fast ion transport (Figure S4).</p>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0003">3</a>(a) illustrates the XRD spectrum of the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC, from which the characteristic diffraction peaks of MoS<sub>2</sub> can be identified indicating the successful loading of MoS<sub>2</sub>. Furthermore, compared with the pure Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub>, the (002) peak position of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC shifts down from 2<i>θ</i>=7.2° to 5.6° (Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0003">3a</a>), demonstrating that the disorder and interlayer spacing of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> significantly increased after anchoring with MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC, which is consistent with the SEM results.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0039_R_d91677030e1240" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">39</a></sup></span> Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> with larger layer spacing can offer more sodium storage sites and more fluent ion transport channels. Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0003">3</a>(b) demonstrates the high-resolution XPS spectrum of Mo 3d, where the peaks at wt 228.6 and 231.8 eV can be attributed to Mo 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Mo3d<sub>3/2</sub> of Mo<sup>4+</sup>, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0040_R_d91677030e1260" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Mater. Chem. C">40</a>, <a href="#batt202300391-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0041_R_d91677030e1260" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Energy Environ. Mater.">41</a></sup></span> The remaining diffraction peaks located at 226.1 and 235 eV positions are attributed to S 2 s and Mo<sup>6+</sup>, respectively [40, 41]. There are two pairs of diffraction peaks in the S 2p spectrum, one pair at 161.5 and 162.6 eV are attributed to 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of S<sup>2−[42]</sup> and the other pair at 163.7 and 164.9 eV are assigned to 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of S (Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0003">3c</a>),<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0043_R_d91677030e1280" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Cell Rep. Phys. Sci.">43</a></sup></span> respectively. The presence of S is caused by a small amount of S remaining on the sample surface during the high temperature vulcanization process. The N 1s spectrum displays the peaks of graphitic N (400.7) and pyridine N (398.3 eV),<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0044_R_d91677030e1283" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">44</a>, <a href="#batt202300391-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0045_R_d91677030e1283" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Energy Mater.">45</a></sup></span> which can further increase the electrical conductivity of the composites due to the large number of off-domain electrons available from the lone pairs of electrons they possess (Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0003">3d</a>). In addition, the remaining derivative peak at 394.6 eV in the N 1s spectrum is a Mo 3p diffraction peak.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="batt202300391-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/926a5e70-0eb2-4153-92f8-3fe1605904d7/batt202300391-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/926a5e70-0eb2-4153-92f8-3fe1605904d7/batt202300391-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/62d69ae1-e6ab-43d0-beb4-eb300b757699/batt202300391-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/926a5e70-0eb2-4153-92f8-3fe1605904d7/batt202300391-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202300391-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202300391" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) XRD patterns of the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>, XPS spectra of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC for b) Mo 3d, c) S 2p, d) N 1s.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0004">4</a>(a) shows the CV curve of the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC at 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup> and a voltage range of 0.01–3 V for the original three cycles. There is a large irreversible reduction peak at 0.2 V in the first lap, which can be attributed to electrolyte decomposition forming solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film. The CV curves of the two subsequent turns almost overlap, indicating that the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC electrode can be stabilized quickly. The reduction peak near 0.01 V can be ascribed to the generation of metallic Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S and the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> between the carbon and/or Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> layers. The oxidation peak at 0.075 V is assigned to the detachment of Na<sup>+</sup> from the carbon and/or Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> layer, while the oxidation peak at 1.8 V is indexed to the oxidation of Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S to form MoS<sub>2</sub>. Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0004">4</a>(b) demonstrates the initial three GCD curves of the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC composite at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> with initial charging and discharging reversible capacities of 734 and 926 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, accompanied by an initial Coulombic efficiency of 79.3 %. Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0004">4</a>(c) shows the cycle capability comparison of the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC, Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC exhibited high specific capacity and cycling stability with a remaining specific capacity of 650 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 700 cycles. During the first few cycles, the capacity decreases rapidly due to the large amount of electrolyte consumed to form the SEI film and the fact that the active substances are not fully utilized. With the continuous charging and discharging, the active substance is fully utilized, and the specific capacity rises slowly until the capacity reaches the maximum value after 350 cycles, and then the capacity shows a regular slow decay trend. In comparison, the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> displays an excellent cycling stability but low specific capacity (~100 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). The MoS<sub>2</sub> exhibits a high specific capacity but a fast capacity decay. The Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC demonstrates high specific capacity and cycling stability can be attributed to its excellent composition and flower-like hierarchical structure. Moreover, the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC exhibits a favorable rate performance obtaining average reversible specific capacities of 627, 621, 613, 601, 576, 534, 484, 434 and 383 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2, 0.4, 0.8, 1.5, 3, 6, 12, 20 and 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0004">4d</a>). This can be ascribed to the multistage structure of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC that provides smooth ion transport channels and the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> that provides high electron transport rates. Furthermore, the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC also displays an acceptable cycling stability with a capacity of 558 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was attained after 1000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The capacity maintained at 351 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 2000 stabilization cycles at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The corresponding capacity retention during the 20th to 2000th cycles was 81.4 % accompanied by an average capacity decay of 0.03 % per cycle. As-prepared Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC composite exhibits good sodium storage properties compared to similar materials reported in the literature (Table S1). The superior electrochemical properties of flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC compared to pure Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> (Figure S5) are due to its better component composition and morphological structure.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="batt202300391-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/779c8626-eedb-44eb-98d0-b3ee4c81a95b/batt202300391-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/779c8626-eedb-44eb-98d0-b3ee4c81a95b/batt202300391-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ce7e79df-c749-4fed-9414-8b9b59dabd90/batt202300391-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/779c8626-eedb-44eb-98d0-b3ee4c81a95b/batt202300391-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202300391-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202300391" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) CV curves, b) GCD profiles, c) cycling performance comparison at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, d) rate capability, e) long-term cycling stability at 1 and 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> of the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To further investigate the reason for the excellent electrochemical properties of the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC, the pseudocapacitive behavior was analyzed after 10 cycles of activation at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> as indicated in Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0005">5</a>. Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0005">5</a>(a) shows the CV curve of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC at different sweep speeds from 0.2 to 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The position of the redox peaks shifted very little as the scan rate increased, which demonstrates that the polarization of the electrode materials is very small and it has the ability of rapid electron and ion transfer. The relationship between the peak current <i>i</i> and the scan rate <i>v</i> in the CV curve is <i>i</i>=a<i>v</i><sup>b</sup>,<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0046_R_d91677030e1626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">46</a>-<a href="#batt202300391-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0049_R_d91677030e1626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">49</a></sup></span> where b is a constant between 0.5 and 1.0. The peak area of the CV curve represents the total capacity, which can be divided into diffusion contribution and capacitive contribution according to the equation <i>i</i>(V)=<i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>+<i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0050_R_d91677030e1641" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Rare Met.">50</a>-<a href="#batt202300391-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0052_R_d91677030e1641" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Energy Mater. Adv.">52</a></sup></span> All the <i>b</i>-values calculated based on the peak current of redox peaks are close to 1, indicating that Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC can provide a large contribution rate of pseudo-capacitance capacity (Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0005">5b</a>). The surface-controlled pseudo-capacitance behavior provides a capacity contribution of up to 95.5 % at 0.8 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0005">5c</a>). As the scanning rate was gradually increased from 0.2 to 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity contribution rate provided by the pseudo-capacitor gradually increases from 91.6 % to 95.9 % (Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0005">5d</a>). The Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC shows a higher pseudo-capacitance contribution compared to Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> (Figure S6) thanks to its flower-like hierarchical structure that exposes a larger active surface.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="batt202300391-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8570a95c-389e-43ee-aecd-218285c47ad8/batt202300391-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8570a95c-389e-43ee-aecd-218285c47ad8/batt202300391-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d5485821-b320-4b05-8825-af8d4ec69c06/batt202300391-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8570a95c-389e-43ee-aecd-218285c47ad8/batt202300391-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202300391-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202300391" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Analysis of pseudo-capacitance behavior of the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC: a) CV profiles at different scanning rates, b) <i>b</i>-value, c) capacitive contribution at 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> and d) capacitive contribution at various scanning rates.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In addition, the reasons for the excellent kinetics of the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC were further analyzed via electrochemical impedance spectroscopy. Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0006">6</a>(a) clearly reflects the Nyquist comparison of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub> and flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC. Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> has ultra-high conductivity with a charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of only 4.7 Ω, whereas the theoretically higher specific capacity of MoS<sub>2</sub> has a <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of up to 203.4 Ω suggesting its poor conductivity. The uniform loading of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles on the surface of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanoflakes greatly enhanced the electrical conductivity of MoS<sub>2</sub>, and the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of the resulting flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC is reduced to 73.4 Ω, which is attributed to the fact that Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> has a high electrical conductivity favoring rapid electron transfer. Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0006">6</a>(b) shows the <i>Z</i>′-<i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup> linear relationship plots in the low frequency section, and the slope value of the fitted line are the Warburg coefficient (<i>σ</i><sub><i>ω</i></sub>) of the electrode materials. The <i>σ</i><sub><i>ω</i></sub> values of MoS<sub>2</sub>, Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> and the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC are 984, 433 and 337, respectively. The flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC takes on the smallest <i>σ</i><sub><i>ω</i></sub> value implying that it owns the fastest ion diffusion rate, which is attributed to its 3D flower-like structure provides a smooth ion transport channel for rapid ionic diffusion. Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC possesses fast charge transfer kinetics and high ion diffusion capabilities that are conducive to its excellent rate performance and stable reversible cycling.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="batt202300391-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1e24be26-75e6-483b-b75b-ffb7a1fef91a/batt202300391-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1e24be26-75e6-483b-b75b-ffb7a1fef91a/batt202300391-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c118530e-a090-4fb1-9eae-5177c0f7b8a2/batt202300391-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1e24be26-75e6-483b-b75b-ffb7a1fef91a/batt202300391-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202300391-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202300391" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Nyquist plots and b) the Z′-ω<sup>−1/2</sup> plots of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub> and the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To further study the practical potential of the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC in practical applications, it was matched with commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and assembled into a full battery to further evaluate its electrochemical performance, as shown in Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0007">7</a>. The sodium storage properties of commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> are shown in Figure S7, and it has excellent cyclic stability. Before assembling a full cell, the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC is assembled into a half cell and subjected to 200 cycles activation at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> to achieve a basically stable state. Then, full cell assembly is performed according to the charge matching principle. The capacities of the full cells are determined based on the sum of the masses of the cathode and anode active substances, which are in a mass ratio of approximately 6 : 1. The full battery achieved a reversible specific capacity of 61 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 120 cycles at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> corresponding to a capacity retention of 77 % (Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0007">7a</a>). Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0007">7</a>(b) illustrates the GCD profiles of the full cell for the initial 5 cycles. Its first charging and discharging capacity is 85.9 and 79.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The GCD curves of the 2nd to 5th circles basically overlap indicating that the electrode material can reach stabilization very quickly. Figure <a href="#batt202300391-fig-0007">7</a>(c) shows a prominent long-term cycling property of the full battery at 0.4 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The capacity can be kept at 59 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 180 cycles with a small capacity degradation rate of 0.1 % each cycle. The superior capacity and recyclability of the full battery can be attributed to the excellent composition and flower-like structure of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC anode, which not only enables the utilization of the individual components but also effectively speeds up the ion/electron transport rates and the structural stability of the electrode.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="batt202300391-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d5fa2a37-df24-4814-a1a1-78ab7548f631/batt202300391-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d5fa2a37-df24-4814-a1a1-78ab7548f631/batt202300391-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f3bdfbd7-091b-4c72-ae9d-152865aa041e/batt202300391-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d5fa2a37-df24-4814-a1a1-78ab7548f631/batt202300391-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202300391-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202300391" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Sodium storage performance of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC‖Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full battery: a) cycling property at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, b) initial five GCD profiles at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, c) long-term cycling property at 0.4 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="batt202300391-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="batt202300391-sec-0003-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the flower-like Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC composite have been prepared by complexation of (MoO<sub>4</sub>)<sup>2−</sup> and dopamine hydrochloride, in-site polymerization reactions with Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets and specifies sulfidation. The ultra-small MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles are homogeneously anchored on the surface of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheet to form a sandwich-like 2D nanosheet structure, which can not only effectively prevent the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets from re-stacking, but also help to shorten the ion diffusion distance, and also help to improve the conductivity of the overall composites. Moreover, the 3D flower-like structure with abundant edges facilitates rapid electrolyte penetration and provides a high contribution of pseudo-capacitance capacity. When employed as anode for SIBs, the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC demonstrates excellent cycling stability with capacity retention of 81.4 % from 20<sup>th</sup> cycle to 2000<sup>th</sup> cycle at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> and excellent rate capacity of 383 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Meanwhile, the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC‖Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell exhibits a capacity of 59 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.4 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 180 cycles. This work presents a novel strategy to construct Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC composite with flower-like structure, as well as may open a new way to construct other MXene-based composites anode materials for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="batt202300391-sec-0004">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="batt202300391-sec-0004-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202300391-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202300391-sec-0005-title"> Preparation of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> nanosheets</h3>
            
            <p>Few-layer Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> was prepared through a modified etching strategy.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300391-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300391-bib-0036_R_d91677030e2162" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nature">36</a></sup></span> Specifically, 2 g of LiF was gradually added to 40 mL of hydrochloric acid (9 mol L<sup>−1</sup>) with constant stirring until LiF was completely dissolved. Subsequently, 2 g of Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> powder was gradually added to the above mixture and stirred continuously at 45 °C for 40 h. The Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> slurry with multilayer structure was then attained by differential centrifugation and repeated washing with deionized water to pH≈7. The multilayer Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> was transferred to a wide-mouth bottle, and then a quantity of deionized water was added and ultrasonically stripped under Ar protection for 30 minutes to obtain few-layer Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> solution. Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> powder can be obtained by freeze-drying few-layer Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> solution.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202300391-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202300391-sec-0006-title"> Preparation of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub>@Mo/PDA precursor</h3>
            
            <p>Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@Mo/PDA precursors with different Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> contents were prepared by the following strategy. First, different volumes of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> solutions (containing 10, 20 and 40 mg of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>, respectively) were measured and diluted to 40 mL, which was denoted as solution A. Then, 0.618 g of (NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>6</sub>Mo<sub>7</sub>O<sub>24</sub> ⋅ 4H<sub>2</sub>O and 0.2 g of DA were successively dissolved into 40 mL of water to obtain solution B. Afterward, solution B was dropped into solution A to reach a uniform solution, and then 0.5 mL of NH<sub>3</sub>⋅H<sub>2</sub>O was added to the above mixed solution with constant stirring for 12 hours. Finally, the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@Mo/PDA precursors containing different Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> contents were obtained by centrifugation, water washing and freeze-drying.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202300391-sec-0007">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202300391-sec-0007-title"> Preparation of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC composites</h3>
            
            <p>A certain mass of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@Mo/PDA precursor and an excess mass of sublimated sulfur (30 times) were weighed and placed at the lower and upper ends of the airflow in the tube furnace, and the final product Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>@MoS<sub>2</sub>/NC was obtained by heating up to 550 °C for 2 h with a ramping rate of 5 °C min<sup>−1</sup> under N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

         <div class="accordion article-accordion">
            <h2><a class="accordion__control" role="button" aria-controls="d91677030e365" aria-expanded="false">
                  <div tabindex="0" class="section__title" id="support-information-section"><span>Supporting Information</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></div></a></h2>
            <div class="accordion__content" role="region" aria-labelledby="support-information-section" style="display: none;" id="d91677030e365" aria-hidden="false">

    window.addEventListener('load', () => {
        document.querySelectorAll('.admin-bar-popup-menu ul li')?.forEach(function (li) {
